821 <h1>

822Breast Cancer Screening (PDQ)Health Professional Version</h1>

823

824 <div id="cgvBody">

825

826 <div class="summary-sections">

827

828 <nav class="on-this-page" role="navigation">

829

831On This Page</h6>

883

884 <section id="_1">

885

886 <h2>

887Overview</h2>

888

889 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

890<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

891<h6>

892In This Section</h6>

893<ul>

894<li>

895<a href="#_7_toc">

896Screening With Mammography<ul>

897<li>

898<a href="#_10_toc">

899Benefits</a>

900</li>

901<li>

902<a href="#_13_toc">

903Harms</a>

904</li>

905</ul>

906</a>

907</li>

908<li>

909<a href="#_15_toc">

910Clinical Breast Examination (CBE)<ul>

911<li>

912<a href="#_16_toc">

913Benefits</a>

914</li>

915<li>

916<a href="#_20_toc">

917Harms</a>

918</li>

919</ul>

920</a>

921</li>

922<li>

923<a href="#_33_toc">

924Breast Self-Examination (BSE)<ul>

925<li>

926<a href="#_34_toc">

927Benefits</a>

928</li>

929<li>

930<a href="#_37_toc">

931Harms</a>

932</li>

933</ul>

934</a>

935</li>

936</ul>

937</nav>

938<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

939<a href="/types/breast/patient/breast-screening-pdq">

940Go to Patient Version</a>

941</div>

942<p id="_3" tabindex="-1">

943Note: The Overview section summarizes the published evidence on this topic. The rest of the summary describes the evidence in more detail.</p>

944<p id="_835" tabindex="-1">

945Other PDQ summaries with information related to breast cancer screening include the following:</p>

946<div class="pdq-content-list">

947<ul id="_836">

948<li>

949<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-prevention-pdq">

950Breast Cancer Prevention</a>

951</li>

952<li>

953<a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-treatment-pdq">

954Breast Cancer Treatment</a>

955</li>

956<li>

957<a href="/types/breast/hp/male-breast-treatment-pdq">

958Male Breast Cancer Treatment</a>

959</li>

960<li>

961<a href="/types/breast/hp/pregnancy-breast-treatment-pdq">

962Breast Cancer Treatment During Pregnancy</a>

963</li>

964</ul>

965</div>

966<p id="_207" tabindex="-1">

967Mammography is the most widely used screening modality for the detection of breast cancer. There is evidence that it decreases breast cancer mortality in women aged 50 to 69 years and that it is associated with harms, including the detection of clinically insignificant cancers that pose no threat to life (overdiagnosis). The benefit of mammography for women aged 40 to 49 years is uncertain.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9681</a>

969,<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9702</a>

971] Randomized trials in India, Iran, and Egypt have studied the use of clinical breast examination (CBE) as a screening test. Some of these studies suggested a shift in late-stage disease; however, there is still insufficient evidence to conclude a mortality benefit.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9723</a>

973-<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

9748</a>

975] Breast self-examination has been shown to have no mortality benefit.</p>

976<p id="_769" tabindex="-1">

977Technologies such as ultrasound, magnetic resonance imaging, and molecular breast imaging are being evaluated, usually as adjuncts to mammography. They are not primary screening tools in the average population.</p>

978<p id="_712" tabindex="-1">

979Informed medical decision making is increasingly recommended for individuals who are considering cancer screening. Many different types and formats of decision aids have been studied. For more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/screening/hp-screening-overview-pdq">

980Cancer Screening Overview</a>

981.</p>

982<section id="_7">

983<h3 id="_7_toc">

984Screening With Mammography</h3>

985<section id="_10">

986<h4 id="_10_toc">

987Benefits</h4>

988<p id="_701" tabindex="-1">

989Randomized controlled trials (RCTs) initiated 50 years ago provide evidence that screening mammography reduces breast cancer&#8211;specific mortality for women aged 60 to 69 years (solid evidence) and women aged 50 to 59 years (fair evidence). Population-based studies done more recently raise questions about the benefits for populations who participate in screening for longer time periods.</p>

990<p id="_702" tabindex="-1">

991<strong>

992Magnitude of Effect:</strong>

993 Based on a meta-analysis of RCTs, the number of women needed to invite for screening to prevent one breast cancer death depends on the woman&#8217;s age: for women aged 39 to 49 years, 1,904 women needed (95% confidence interval [CI], 929&#8211;6,378); for women aged 50 to 59 years, 1,339 women needed (95% CI, 322&#8211;7,455);

994and for women aged 60 to 69 years, 377 women needed (95% CI, 230&#8211;1,050).[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

9959</a>

996]</p>

997<div class="pdq-content-list">

998<ul id="_24" class="pdq-address-block">

999<li>

1000<strong>

1001Study Design</strong>

1002: RCTs, population-based evidence.</li>

1003<li>

1004<strong>

1005Internal Validity</strong>

1006: Variable, but meta-analysis of RCTs is good.</li>

1007<li>

1008<strong>

1009Consistency</strong>

1010: Poor.</li>

1011<li>

1012<strong>

1013External Validity</strong>

1014: Uncertain.</li>

1015</ul>

1016</div>

1017<p id="_704" tabindex="-1">

1018The validity of meta-analyses of RCT demonstrating a mortality benefit is limited by improvements in medical imaging and treatment in the decades since their completion. The 25-year follow-up from the Canadian National Breast Screening Study (CNBSS),[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

101910</a>

1020] completed in 2014, showed no mortality benefit associated with screening mammograms.</p>

1021</section>

1022<section id="_13">

1023<h4 id="_13_toc">

1024Harms</h4>

1025<p id="_14" tabindex="-1">

1026Based on solid evidence, screening mammography may lead to the following harms: </p>

1027<div class="pdq-content-list">

1028<ul id="_25">

1029<li>

1030<strong>

1031Overdiagnosis and Resulting Treatment of Insignificant Cancers</strong>

1032: Some screen-detected cancers are life threatening and others are not, with no definitive way of discriminating between them. Therefore, standard cancer therapies, including surgery, radiation, endocrine therapy, chemotherapy, and therapies targeting the <em class="gene-name">

1033HER2</em>

1034 receptor, are recommended for all cases, even for patients who will not benefit.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1035<ul id="_26" class="pdq-address-block">

1036<li>

1037<strong>

1038Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1039: Between 20% and 50% of screen-detected cancers represent overdiagnosis based on patient age, life expectancy, and tumor type (ductal carcinoma <em>

1040in situ</em>

1041 and/or invasive).[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

104211</a>

1043,<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

104412</a>

1045] These estimates are based on two imperfect analytic methods:[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

104611</a>

1047,<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

104813</a>

1049]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1050<ol id="_705">

1051<li>

1052Long-term follow-up of RCTs of screening.</li>

1053<li>

1054The calculation of excess incidence in large screening programs.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

105511</a>

1056,<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

105712</a>

1058]</li>

1059</ol>

1060</div>

1061</li>

1062<li>

1063<strong>

1064Study Design</strong>

1065: RCTs, descriptive, population-based comparisons, autopsy series, and series of mammary reduction specimens.</li>

1066</ul>

1067</div>

1068</li>

1069<li>

1070<strong>

1071False Positives With Additional Testing and Anxiety.</strong>

1072<div class="pdq-content-list">

1073<ul id="_27" class="pdq-address-block">

1074<li>

1075<strong>

1076Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1077: In the United States, approximately 10% of women are recalled for further testing after a screening examination. However, only 0.5% of tested women have cancer. Thus, approximately 9.5% of tested women have a false-positive exam.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

107814</a>

1079,<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

108015</a>

1081] Approximately 50% of women screened annually for 10 years in the United States experience a false-positive exam; of these, 7% to 17% will undergo biopsies.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

108216</a>

1083,<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

108417</a>

1085] Additional testing is less likely when prior mammograms are available for comparison.</li>

1086<li>

1087<strong>

1088Study Design</strong>

1089: Descriptive, population-based.</li>

1090</ul>

1091</div>

1092</li>

1093<li>

1094<strong>

1095False Negatives With False Sense of Security and Potential Delay in Cancer Diagnosis.</strong>

1096<div class="pdq-content-list">

1097<ul id="_28" class="pdq-address-block">

1098<li>

1099<strong>

1100Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1101: Invasive breast cancer is present but undetected by mammography (false negative) in 6% to 46% of exams. False-negative exams are more likely for mucinous and lobular types of cancer and for rapidly growing <em>

1102interval</em>

1103 tumors, which become detectable between regular mammograms and in dense breasts, which are common in younger women.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

110418</a>

1105-<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

110620</a>

1107]</li>

1108<li>

1109<strong>

1110Study Design</strong>

1111: Descriptive, population-based.</li>

1112</ul>

1113</div>

1114</li>

1115<li>

1116<strong>

1117Radiation-Induced Breast Cancer</strong>

1118: Radiation-induced mutations occur with radiation doses higher than those used in a single mammography examination, so the exposure associated with a typical two-view mammogram is extremely unlikely to cause cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

111921</a>

1120,<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

112122</a>

1122]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1123<ul id="_29" class="pdq-address-block">

1124<li>

1125<strong>

1126Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1127: Theoretically, annual mammograms in women aged 40 to 80 years may cause up to one breast cancer per 1,000 women.[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

112821</a>

1129,<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

113022</a>

1131]</li>

1132<li>

1133<strong>

1134Study Design</strong>

1135: Descriptive, population-based.</li>

1136</ul>

1137</div>

1138</li>

1139</ul>

1140</div>

1141<p id="_30" tabindex="-1">

1142For all of these conclusions regarding potential harms from screening mammography, internal validity, consistency, and external validity are good.</p>

1143</section>

1144</section>

1145<section id="_15">

1146<h3 id="_15_toc">

1147Clinical Breast Examination (CBE)</h3>

1148<section id="_16">

1149<h4 id="_16_toc">

1150Benefits</h4>

1151<p id="_17" tabindex="-1">

1152The CNBSS trial did not study the efficacy of CBE versus no screening. Ongoing randomized trials, two in India and one in Egypt, are designed to assess the efficacy of screening CBE but have not reported mortality data.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11533</a>

1154-<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

11558</a>

1156] Thus, the efficacy of screening CBE cannot be assessed yet.</p>

1157<div class="pdq-content-list">

1158<ul id="_31" class="pdq-address-block">

1159<li>

1160<strong>

1161Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1162: The current evidence is insufficient to assess the additional benefits and harms of CBE. The single RCT comparing high-quality CBE with screening mammography showed equivalent benefit. CBE accuracy in the community setting might be lower than in the RCT.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

11633</a>

1164-<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11656</a>

1166]</li>

1167<li>

1168<strong>

1169Study Design</strong>

1170: Single RCT, population cohort studies.</li>

1171<li>

1172<strong>

1173Internal Validity</strong>

1174: Good.</li>

1175<li>

1176<strong>

1177Consistency and External Validity</strong>

1178: Poor.</li>

1179</ul>

1180</div>

1181</section>

1182<section id="_20">

1183<h4 id="_20_toc">

1184Harms</h4>

1185<p id="_21" tabindex="-1">

1186Screening by CBE may lead to the following harms: </p>

1187<div class="pdq-content-list">

1188<ul id="_22">

1189<li>

1190<strong>

1191False Positives With Additional Testing and Anxiety.</strong>

1192<div class="pdq-content-list">

1193<ul id="_23" class="pdq-address-block">

1194<li>

1195<strong>

1196Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1197: Specificity in women aged 50 to 59 years was 88% to 99%, yielding a false-positive rate of 1% to 12% for all women screened.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

119823</a>

1199]</li>

1200<li>

1201<strong>

1202Study Design</strong>

1203: Descriptive, population based.</li>

1204<li>

1205<strong>

1206Internal Validity, Consistency, and External Validity</strong>

1207: Good.</li>

1208</ul>

1209</div>

1210</li>

1211<li>

1212<strong>

1213False Negatives With Potential False Reassurance and Delay in Cancer Diagnosis.</strong>

1214<div class="pdq-content-list">

1215<ul id="_32" class="pdq-address-block">

1216<li>

1217<strong>

1218Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1219: Of women with cancer, 17% to 43% have a negative CBE. Sensitivity is higher with longer duration and higher quality of the examination by trained personnel.</li>

1220<li>

1221<strong>

1222Study Design</strong>

1223: Descriptive, population based.</li>

1224<li>

1225<strong>

1226Internal and External Validity</strong>

1227: Good.</li>

1228<li>

1229<strong>

1230Consistency</strong>

1231: Fair.</li>

1232</ul>

1233</div>

1234</li>

1235</ul>

1236</div>

1237</section>

1238</section>

1239<section id="_33">

1240<h3 id="_33_toc">

1241Breast Self-Examination (BSE)</h3>

1242<section id="_34">

1243<h4 id="_34_toc">

1244Benefits</h4>

1245<p id="_35" tabindex="-1">

1246BSE has been compared with no screening and has been shown to have no benefit in reducing breast cancer mortality.</p>

1247<div class="pdq-content-list">

1248<ul id="_36" class="pdq-address-block">

1249<li>

1250<strong>

1251Magnitude of Effect</strong>

1252: No effect.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

125324</a>

1254,<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

125525</a>

1256]</li>

1257<li>

1258<strong>

1259Study Design</strong>

1260: Two RCTs.</li>

1261<li>

1262<strong>

1263Internal Validity and Consistency</strong>

1264: Fair.</li>

1265<li>

1266<strong>

1267External Validity</strong>

1268: Poor.</li>

1269</ul>

1270</div>

1271</section>

1272<section id="_37">

1273<h4 id="_37_toc">

1274Harms</h4>

1275<p id="_38" tabindex="-1">

1276There is solid evidence that formal instruction and encouragement to perform BSE leads to more breast biopsies and more diagnoses of benign breast lesions.</p>

1277<div class="pdq-content-list">

1278<ul id="_39" class="pdq-address-block">

1279<li>

1280<strong>

1281Magnitude of Effects on Health Outcomes</strong>

1282: Biopsy rate was 1.8% among the study population, compared with 1.0% among the control group.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

128324</a>

1284]</li>

1285<li>

1286<strong>

1287Study Design</strong>

1288: Two RCTs, cohort studies.</li>

1289<li>

1290<strong>

1291Internal Validity</strong>

1292: Good.</li>

1293<li>

1294<strong>

1295Consistency</strong>

1296: Fair.</li>

1297<li>

1298<strong>

1299External Validity</strong>

1300: Poor.</li>

1301</ul>

1302</div>

1303</section>

1473<section id="_41">

1474<h3 id="_41_toc">

1475Breast Cancer Incidence and Mortality</h3>

1476<p id="_43" tabindex="-1">

1477Breast cancer is the most common noncutaneous cancer in U.S. women, with an estimated 297,790 cases of invasive disease, 55,720 cases of <em>

1478in situ</em>

1479 disease, and 43,170 deaths expected in 2023.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

14801</a>

1481] Women with inherited risk, including <em class="gene-name">

1482BRCA1</em>

1483 and <em class="gene-name">

1484BRCA2</em>

1485 gene carriers, make up approximately 5% to 10% of breast cancer cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

14862</a>

1487] Men account for about 1% of breast cancer cases and breast cancer deaths.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

14881</a>

1489]</p>

1490<p id="_649" tabindex="-1">

1491The biggest risk factor for breast cancer is being female followed by advancing age. Other risk factors include hormonal aspects (such as early menarche, late menopause, nulliparity, late first pregnancy, and postmenopausal hormone therapy use), alcohol consumption, and exposure to ionizing radiation.</p>

1492<p id="_650" tabindex="-1">

1493Breast cancer incidence in White women is higher than in Black women, who also have a lower survival rate for every stage when diagnosed. This may reflect differences in screening behavior and access to health care. Hispanic women, Asian or Pacific Islander women, and American Indian or Alaska Native women have lower incidence and mortality rates than White or Black women.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

14943</a>

1495]</p>

1496<p id="_651" tabindex="-1">

1497Breast cancer incidence depends on reproductive issues (such as early vs. late pregnancy, multiparity, and breastfeeding), participation in screening, and postmenopausal hormone usage. The incidence of breast cancer (especially ductal carcinoma <em>

1498in situ</em>

1499 [DCIS]) increased dramatically after mammography was widely adopted in the United States and the United Kingdom.[<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

15004</a>

1501] Widespread use of postmenopausal hormone therapy was associated with a dramatic increase in breast cancer incidence, a trend that reversed when its use decreased.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15025</a>

1503]</p>

1504<p id="_706" tabindex="-1">

1505In any population, the adoption of screening is not followed by a decline in the incidence of advanced-stage cancer.</p>

1506</section>

1507<section id="_560">

1508<h3 id="_560_toc">

1509Evaluation of Breast Symptoms</h3>

1510<p id="_561" tabindex="-1">

1511Women with breast symptoms undergo diagnostic mammography as opposed to screening mammography, which is done in asymptomatic women. In a 10-year study of breast symptoms prompting medical attention, a breast mass led to a cancer diagnosis in 10.7% of cases, whereas pain was associated with cancer in only 1.8% of cases.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

15126</a>

1513]</p>

1514</section>

1515<section id="_4">

1516<h3 id="_4_toc">

1517Pathological Evaluation of Breast Tissue</h3>

1518<section id="_64">

1519<h4 id="_64_toc">

1520Invasive breast cancer</h4>

1521<p id="_65" tabindex="-1">

1522Breast cancer can be diagnosed when breast tissue cells removed during a biopsy are studied microscopically. The breast tissue to be sampled can be identified by an abnormality on an imaging study or because it is palpable. Breast biopsies can be performed with a thin needle attached to a syringe (fine-needle aspirate), a larger needle (core biopsy), or by excision (excisional biopsy). Image guidance can improve accuracy. Needle biopsies sample an abnormal area large enough to make a diagnosis. Excisional biopsies aim to remove the entire region of abnormality.</p>

1523</section>

1524<section id="_66">

1525<h4 id="_66_toc">

1526Ductal carcinoma <em>

1527in situ</em>

1528 (DCIS)</h4>

1529<p id="_67" tabindex="-1">

1530DCIS is a noninvasive condition that can be associated with, or evolve into, invasive cancer, with variable frequency and time course.[<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

15317</a>

1532] Some authors include DCIS with invasive breast cancer statistics, but others argue that it would be better if the term were replaced with ductal intraepithelial neoplasia, similar to the terminology used for cervical and prostate precursor lesions, and that excluding DCIS from breast cancer statistics should be considered.</p>

1533<p id="_68" tabindex="-1">

1534DCIS is most often diagnosed by mammography. In the United States, only 4,900 women were diagnosed with DCIS in 1983 before the adoption of mammography screening, compared with approximately 55,720 women who are expected to be diagnosed in 2023.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

15351</a>

1536,<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

15377</a>

1538,<a href="#cit/section_2.8">

15398</a>

1540] The Canadian National Breast Screening Study-2, which evaluated women aged 50 to 59 years, found a fourfold increase in DCIS cases in women screened by clinical breast examination (CBE) plus mammography, compared with those screened by CBE alone, with no difference in breast cancer mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_2.9">

15419</a>

1542] For more information, see <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-treatment-pdq">

1543Breast Cancer Treatment</a>

1544.</p>

1545<p id="_69" tabindex="-1">

1546The natural history of DCIS is poorly understood because nearly all DCIS cases are detected by screening and nearly all are treated. Development of breast cancer after treatment of DCIS depends on the pathological characteristics of the lesion and on the treatment. In a randomized trial, 13.4% of women whose DCIS was excised by lumpectomy developed ipsilateral invasive breast cancer within 90 months, compared with 3.9% of those treated by both lumpectomy and radiation.[<a href="#cit/section_2.10">

154710</a>

1548] Among women diagnosed and treated for DCIS, the percentage of women who died of breast cancer is lower than that for the age-matched population at large.[<a href="#cit/section_2.11">

154911</a>

1550,<a href="#cit/section_2.12">

155112</a>

1552] This favorable outcome may reflect the benign nature of the condition, the benefits of treatment, or the volunteer effect (i.e., women who undergo breast cancer screening are generally healthier than those who do not do so).</p>

1553</section>

1554<section id="_562">

1555<h4 id="_562_toc">

1556Atypia</h4>

1557<p id="_563" tabindex="-1">

1558Atypia, which is a risk factor for breast cancer, is found in 4% to 10% of breast biopsies.[<a href="#cit/section_2.13">

155913</a>

1560,<a href="#cit/section_2.14">

156114</a>

1562] Atypia is a diagnostic classification with considerable variation among practicing pathologists.[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

156315</a>

1564]</p>

1565</section>

1566<section id="_564">

1567<h4 id="_564_toc">

1568Variability of pathologists&#8217; diagnoses on the interpretation of breast biopsy specimens</h4>

1569<p id="_565" tabindex="-1">

1570The range of pathologists' diagnoses of breast tissue includes benign without atypia, atypia, DCIS, and invasive breast cancer. The incidence of atypia and DCIS breast lesions has increased over the past three decades as a result of widespread mammography screening, although atypia is generally mammographically occult.[<a href="#cit/section_2.16">

157116</a>

1572,<a href="#cit/section_2.17">

157317</a>

1574] Misclassification of breast lesions may contribute to either overtreatment or undertreatment of lesions&#8212;with variability especially in the diagnoses of atypia and DCIS.[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

157515</a>

1576,<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

157718</a>

1578-<a href="#cit/section_2.22">

157922</a>

1580]</p>

1581<p id="_609" tabindex="-1">

1582 The largest study on this topic, the B-Path study, involved 115 practicing U.S. pathologists who interpreted a single-breast biopsy slide per case, and it compared their interpretations with an expert consensus-derived reference diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

158315</a>

1584] While the overall agreement between the individual pathologists&#8217; interpretations and the expert reference diagnoses was highest for invasive carcinoma, there were markedly lower levels of agreement for DCIS and atypia.[<a href="#cit/section_2.15">

158515</a>

1586] As the B-Path study included higher proportions of cases of atypia and DCIS than typically seen in clinical practice, the authors expanded their work by applying Bayes&#8217; theorem to estimate how diagnostic variability affects accuracy from the perspective of a U.S. woman aged 50 to 59 years having a breast biopsy.[<a href="#cit/section_2.18">

158718</a>

1588] At the U.S. population level, it is estimated that 92.3% (95% confidence interval [CI], 91.4%&#8211;93.1%) of breast biopsy diagnoses would be verified by an expert reference consensus diagnosis, with 4.6% (95% CI, 3.9%&#8211;5.3%) of initial breast biopsies estimated to be overinterpreted and 3.2% (95% CI, 2.7%&#8211;3.6%) under interpreted. <a href="/types/breast/hp/breast-screening-pdq#_610">

1589Figure 1</a>

1590 shows the predicted outcomes per 100 breast biopsies, overall and by diagnostic category.</p>

1591<figure id="figure_610" class="image-center">

1592<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/784729.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1593Enlarge</a>

1594<img id="_610" alt="Charts showing the predicted outcomes for 100 breast biopsies, overall and by diagnostic category." title="Charts showing the predicted outcomes for 100 breast biopsies, overall and by diagnostic category." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/784729-750.jpg">

1595<figcaption class="caption-container">

1596Figure 1. Predicted outcomes per 100 breast biopsies, overall and by diagnostic category. From Annals of Internal Medicine, Elmore JG, Nelson HD, Pepe MS, Longton GM, Tosteson AN, Geller B, Onega T, Carney PA, Jackson SL, Allison KH, Weaver DL, Variability in Pathologists' Interpretations of Individual Breast Biopsy Slides: A Population Perspective, Volume 164, Issue 10, Pages 649&#8211;55, Copyright &#169; 2016 American College of Physicians. All Rights Reserved. Reprinted with the permission of American College of Physicians, Inc.</figcaption>

1597</figure>

1598<p id="_575" tabindex="-1">

1599To address the high rates of discordance in breast tissue diagnosis, laboratory policies that require second opinions are becoming more common. A national survey of 252 breast pathologists participating in the B-Path study found that 65% of respondents reported having a laboratory policy that requires second opinions for all cases initially diagnosed as invasive disease. Additionally, 56% of respondents reported policies that require second opinions for initial diagnoses of DCIS, while 36% of respondents reported mandatory second opinion policies for cases initially diagnosed as atypical ductal hyperplasia.[<a href="#cit/section_2.23">

160023</a>

1601] In this same survey, pathologists overwhelmingly agreed that second opinions improved diagnostic accuracy (96%).</p>

1602<p id="_608" tabindex="-1">

1603A simulation study that used B-Path study data evaluated 12 strategies for obtaining second opinions to improve interpretation of breast histopathology.[<a href="#cit/section_2.24">

160424</a>

1605] Accuracy improved significantly with all second-opinion strategies, except for the strategy limiting second opinions only to cases of invasive cancer. Accuracy improved regardless of the pathologists&#8217; confidence in their diagnosis or their level of experience. While the second opinions improved accuracy, they did not completely eliminate diagnostic variability, especially in the challenging case of breast atypia.</p>

1606</section>

1607</section>

1608<section id="_123">

1609<h3 id="_123_toc">

1610Special Populations</h3>

1611<section id="_143">

1612<h4 id="_143_toc">

1613Women at increased risk who may benefit more from screening </h4>

1614<section id="_661">

1615<h5 id="_661_toc">

1616Women with <em class="gene-name">

1617BRCA1</em>

1618 and <em class="gene-name">

1619BRCA2</em>

1620 genetic mutations</h5>

1621<p id="_662" tabindex="-1">

1622Women with an increased risk of breast cancer caused by a <em class="gene-name">

1623BRCA1</em>

1624 or <em class="gene-name">

1625BRCA2</em>

1626 genetic mutation might benefit from increased screening. For more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/brca-genes-hp-pdq">

1627BRCA1 and BRCA2: Cancer Risks and Management</a>

1628.</p>

1629</section>

1630<section id="_663">

1631<h5 id="_663_toc">

1632Recipients of thoracic radiation</h5>

1633</section>

1634<p id="_675" tabindex="-1">

1635Women with Hodgkin and non-Hodgkin lymphoma who were treated with mantle irradiation have an increased risk of breast cancer, starting 10 years after completing therapy and continuing life-long. Therefore, screening mammography has been advocated, even though it may begin at a relatively young age.[<a href="#cit/section_2.25">

163625</a>

1637,<a href="#cit/section_2.26">

163826</a>

1639]</p>

1640</section>

1641<section id="_664">

1642<h4 id="_664_toc">

1643Individuals who benefit less from screening</h4>

1644<section id="_676">

1645<h5 id="_676_toc">

1646Women with limited life expectancy</h5>

1647<p id="_677" tabindex="-1">

1648The potential benefits of screening mammography occur well after the examination, often many years later, whereas the harms occur immediately. Therefore, women with limited life expectancy and comorbidities who suffer harms may do so without benefit. Nonetheless, many of these women undergo screening mammography.[<a href="#cit/section_2.27">

164927</a>

1650] In one study, approximately 9% of women with advanced cancer underwent cancer screening tests.[<a href="#cit/section_2.28">

165128</a>

1652]</p>

1653</section>

1654<section id="_678">

1655<h5 id="_678_toc">

1656Older women</h5>

1657<p id="_679" tabindex="-1">

1658Screening mammography may yield cancer diagnoses in approximately 1% of women aged 66 to 79 years, but most of these cancers are low risk.[<a href="#cit/section_2.29">

165929</a>

1660] The question remains whether the diagnosis and treatment of localized breast cancer in older women is beneficial.</p>

1661</section>

1662<section id="_680">

1663<h5 id="_680_toc">

1664Young women</h5>

1665<p id="_681" tabindex="-1">

1666There is no evidence of benefit in performing screening mammography in average-risk women younger than 40 years.</p>

1667</section>

1668<section id="_682">

1669<h5 id="_682_toc">

1670Men</h5>

1671<p id="_683" tabindex="-1">

1672Approximately 1% of all breast cancers occur in men.[<a href="#cit/section_2.30">

167330</a>

1674] Most cases are diagnosed during the evaluation of palpable lesions, which are generally easy to detect. Treatment consists of surgery, radiation, and systemic adjuvant hormone therapy or chemotherapy. For more information, see <a href="/types/breast/hp/male-breast-treatment-pdq">

1675Male Breast Cancer Treatment</a>

1676. In this population, screening is unlikely to be beneficial.</p>

1677</section>

1678</section>

BMJ 353: i3069, 2016.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27334105&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27334105&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1777[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1778</li>

1779<li id="section_2.25">

1780Mariscotti G, Belli P, Bernardi D, et al.: Mammography and MRI for screening women who underwent chest radiation therapy (lymphoma survivors): recommendations for surveillance from the Italian College of Breast Radiologists by SIRM. Radiol Med 121 (11): 834-837, 2016.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27406629&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=27406629&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1797[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

Lancet 367 (9510): 595-604, 2006.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=16488803&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=16488803&amp;dopt=Abstract">
