2905</section>

2906<section id="_412">

2907<h4 id="_412_toc">

2908False negatives leading to a false sense of security</h4>

2909<p id="_413" tabindex="-1">

2910The sensitivity of mammography ranges from 70% to 90%, depending on characteristics of the interpreting radiologist (level of experience) and characteristics of the woman (age, breast density, hormone status, and diet). Assuming an average sensitivity of 80%, mammograms will miss approximately 20% of the breast cancers that are present at the time of screening (false negatives). Many of these missed cancers are high risk, with adverse biological characteristics. If a <em>

2911normal</em>

2912 mammogram dissuades or postpones a woman or her doctor from evaluating breast symptoms, she may suffer adverse consequences. Thus, a negative mammogram should never dissuade a woman or her physician from additional evaluation of breast symptoms.</p>

2913</section>

2914<section id="_429">

2915<h4 id="_429_toc">

2916Discomfort</h4>

2917<p id="_430" tabindex="-1">

2918Positioning of the woman and breast compression reduce motion artifact and improve mammogram image quality. Pain and/or discomfort was reported by 90% of women undergoing mammography, with 12% of women rating the sensation as intense or intolerable.[<a href="#cit/section_3.129">

2919129</a>

2920] A systematic review of 22 studies investigating mammography-associated pain and discomfort found wide variations, some of which were associated with menstrual cycle stage, anxiety, and premammography anticipation of pain.[<a href="#cit/section_3.130">

2921130</a>

2922]</p>

2923</section>

2924<section id="_414">

2925<h4 id="_414_toc">

2926Radiation exposure</h4>

2927<p id="_415" tabindex="-1">

2928The major risk factors for radiation-associated breast cancer are young age at exposure and dose; however, rarely there are women with an inherited susceptibility to radiation-induced damage who must avoid radiation exposure at any age.[<a href="#cit/section_3.131">

2929131</a>

2930,<a href="#cit/section_3.132">

2931132</a>

2932] For many women older than 40 years, the likely benefits of screening mammography outweigh the risks.[<a href="#cit/section_3.133">

2933133</a>

2934,<a href="#cit/section_3.131">

2935131</a>

2936,<a href="#cit/section_3.134">

2937134</a>

2938] Standard two-view screening mammography exposes the breasts to a mean dose of 4 mSv, and the whole body to 0.29 mSv.[<a href="#cit/section_3.132">

2939132</a>

2940,<a href="#cit/section_3.135">

2941135</a>

2942] Thus, up to one breast cancer may be induced per 1,000 women undergoing annual mammograms from ages 40 to 80 years. Such risk is doubled in women with large breasts who require increased radiation doses and in women with breast augmentation who require additional views. Radiation-induced breast cancers may be reduced fivefold for women who begin biennial screening at age 50 years rather than annually at age 40 years.[<a href="#cit/section_3.136">

2943136</a>

2944]</p>

2945</section>

2946<section id="_431">

2947<h4 id="_431_toc">

2948Psychological harms of false positives</h4>

2949<p id="_432" tabindex="-1">

2950A telephone survey of 308 women performed 3 months after screening mammography revealed that about one-fourth of the 68 women recalled for additional testing were still experiencing worry that affected their mood or functioning, even though that testing had ruled out cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_3.137">

2951137</a>

2952] Research into whether the psychological impact of a false-positive test is long-standing yields mixed results. A cohort study in Spain in 2002 found immediate psychological impact to a woman after receiving a false-positive mammogram, but these results dissipated within a few months.[<a href="#cit/section_3.138">

2953138</a>

2954] A cohort study in Denmark in 2013 that measured the psychological effects of a false-positive test result several years after the event found long-term negative psychological consequences.[<a href="#cit/section_3.139">

2955139</a>

2956] Several studies have shown that the anxiety after evaluation of a false-positive test leads to increased participation in future screening examinations.[<a href="#cit/section_3.140">

2957140</a>

2958-<a href="#cit/section_3.143">

2959143</a>

2960]</p>

2961</section>

2962<section id="_695">

2963<h4 id="_695_toc">

2964Financial strain and opportunity costs</h4>

2965<p id="_696" tabindex="-1">

2966These potential harms of screening have not been well researched, but it is clear that they exist.</p>

2967</section>

3548

3549 <section id="_543">

3550

3551 <h2>

3552Other Imaging Modalities: Ultrasound, Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI), and Thermography</h2>

3553

3554 <div id="_section_543" class="pdq-sections">

3555<p id="_709" tabindex="-1">

3556<strong>

3557Ultrasound</strong>

3558</p>

3559<p id="_544" tabindex="-1">

3560Ultrasound is used for the diagnostic evaluation of palpable or mammographically identified masses, rather than serving as a primary screening modality. A review of the literature and expert opinion by the European Group for Breast Cancer Screening concluded that &#8220;there is little evidence to support the use of ultrasound in population breast cancer screening at any age.&#8221;[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

35611</a>

3562] The Japan Strategic Anti-cancer Randomized Trial (J-START) is a screening trial that randomly assigned women aged 40 to 49 years to either mammography and ultrasound screening (intervention group) or mammography screening alone (control group). The initial results of this trial indicated that supplemental screening with ultrasound (i.e., mammography + ultrasound versus mammography alone) increased the detection rate of early-stage breast cancers, but its effect on mortality is not clear at this time.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

35632</a>

3564]</p>

3565<p id="_710" tabindex="-1">

3566<strong>

3567Breast MRI</strong>

3568</p>

3569<p id="_692" tabindex="-1">

3570Breast MRI is used in women for diagnostic evaluation, including evaluating the integrity of silicone breast implants, assessing palpable masses after surgery or radiation therapy, detecting mammographically and sonographically occult breast cancer in patients with axillary nodal metastasis, and preoperative planning for some patients with known breast cancer. There is no ionizing radiation exposure with this procedure. MRI has been promoted as a screening test for breast cancer among women at elevated risk of breast cancer based on <em class="gene-name">

3571BRCA1/2</em>

3572 mutation carriers, a strong family history of breast cancer, or several genetic syndromes, such as Li-Fraumeni syndrome or Cowden disease.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

35733</a>

3574-<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

35755</a>

3576] Breast MRI is more sensitive but less specific than screening mammography [<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

35776</a>

3578,<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

35797</a>

3580] and is up to 35 times as expensive.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

35818</a>

3582-<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

358312</a>

3584]</p>

3585<p id="_711" tabindex="-1">

3586<strong>

3587Thermography</strong>

3588</p>

3589<p id="_548" tabindex="-1">

3590Using infrared imaging techniques, thermography of the breast identifies temperature changes in the skin as a possible indicator of an underlying tumor, displaying these changes in color patterns. Thermographic devices have been approved by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration under the 510(k) process, but no randomized trials have compared thermography to other screening modalities. Small cohort studies do not suggest any additional benefit for the use of thermography as an adjunct modality.[<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

359113</a>

3592,<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

359314</a>

3594]</p>

3663 <h2>

3664Nonimaging Screening Modalities</h2>

3665

3666 <div id="_section_121" class="pdq-sections">

3667<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

3668<h6>

3669In This Section</h6>

3670<ul>

3671<li>

3672<a href="#_187_toc">

3673Clinical Breast Examination</a>

3674</li>

3675<li>

3676<a href="#_190_toc">

3677Breast Self-Examination (BSE)</a>

3678</li>

3679<li>

3681Tissue Sampling (Fine-Needle Aspiration, Nipple Aspirate, Ductal Lavage)</a>

3685<section id="_187">

3686<h3 id="_187_toc">

3687Clinical Breast Examination</h3>

3688<p id="_188" tabindex="-1">

3689The effect of screening clinical breast examination (CBE) on breast cancer mortality has not been fully established. The Canadian National Breast Screening Study (CNBSS) compared high-quality CBE plus mammography with CBE alone in women aged 50 to 59 years. CBE, lasting 5 to 10 minutes per breast, was conducted by trained health professionals, with periodic evaluations of performance quality. The frequency of cancer diagnosis, stage, interval cancers, and breast cancer mortality were similar in the two groups and similar to outcomes with mammography alone.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

36901</a>

3691] With a mean follow-up of 13 years, breast cancer mortality was similar in the two groups (mortality rate ratio, 1.02; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.78&#8211;1.33).[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

36922</a>

3693] The investigators estimated the operating characteristics for CBE alone; for 19,965 women aged 50 to 59 years, sensitivity was 83%, 71%, 57%, 83%, and 77% for years 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 of the trial, respectively; specificity ranged between 88% and 96%. Positive predictive value (PPV), which is the proportion of cancers detected per abnormal examination, was estimated to be 3% to 4%. For 25,620 women aged 40 to 49 years who were examined only at entry, the estimated sensitivity was 71%, specificity was 84%, and PPV was 1.5%.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

36943</a>

3695]</p>

3696<p id="_189" tabindex="-1">

3697In clinical trials involving community clinicians, CBE-type screening had higher specificity (97%&#8211;99%) [<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

36984</a>

3699] and lower sensitivity (22%&#8211;36%) than that experienced by examiners.[<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

37005</a>

3701-<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

37028</a>

3703] A study of screening in women with a positive family history of breast cancer showed that, after a normal initial evaluation, the patient herself, or her clinician performing a CBE, identified more cancers than did mammography.[<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

37049</a>

3705]</p>

3706<p id="_708" tabindex="-1">

3707 Another study examined the usefulness of adding CBE to screening mammography; among 61,688 women older than 40 years and screened by mammography and CBE, sensitivity for mammography was 78%, and combined mammography-CBE sensitivity was 82%. Specificity was lower for women undergoing both screening modalities than it was for women undergoing mammography alone (97% vs. 99%).[<a href="#cit/section_5.10">

370810</a>

3709] Another study reported the results of a large cluster randomized controlled trial in India that assessed the efficacy of screening with CBE versus no screening on breast cancer mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

371011</a>

3711] This trial recruited 151,538 women aged 35 to 64 years with no history of breast cancer. After 20 years of follow-up, there was an overall statistically nonsignificant 15% reduction in breast cancer mortality in the screening with CBE arm versus the control arm, but a post hoc subset analysis demonstrated a statistically significant 30% relative reduction in mortality attributable to screening with CBE for women older than 50 years. However, the results of the subset analysis should be interpreted with caution, as this was a cluster randomized trial with only 20 clusters, which raises concerns about potential imbalances between the control and study arms of the trial. Other international trials of CBE are under way, one in India and one in Egypt.</p>

3712</section>

3713<section id="_190">

3714<h3 id="_190_toc">

3715Breast Self-Examination (BSE)</h3>

3716<p id="_191" tabindex="-1">

3717Monthly BSE has been promoted, but there is no evidence that it reduces breast cancer mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

371812</a>

3719,<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

372013</a>

3721] The only large, randomized clinical trial of BSE assigned 266,064 female Shanghai factory workers to either BSE instruction with reinforcement and encouragement, or instruction on the prevention of lower back pain. Neither group underwent any other breast cancer screening. After 10 to 11 years of follow-up, 135 breast cancer deaths occurred in the instruction group, and 131 cancer deaths occurred in the control group (relative risk [RR], 1.04; 95% CI, 0.82&#8211;1.33). Although the number of invasive breast cancers diagnosed in the two groups was about the same, women in the instruction group had more breast biopsies and more benign lesions diagnosed than did women in the control group.[<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

372214</a>

3723]</p>

3724<p id="_192" tabindex="-1">

3725Other research results on BSE come from three trials. First, more than 100,000 Leningrad women were assigned to BSE training or control by cluster randomization; the BSE group training had more breast biopsies without improved breast cancer mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_5.15">

372615</a>

3727] Second, in the United Kingdom Trial of Early Detection of Breast Cancer, more than 63,500 women aged 45 to 64 years were invited to educational sessions about BSE. After 10 years of follow-up, breast cancer mortality rates were similar to the rates in centers without organized BSE education (RR, 1.07; 95% CI, 0.93&#8211;1.22).[<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

372816</a>

3729] Thirdly, in contrast, a case-control study nested within the CNBSS compared self-reported BSE frequency before enrollment with breast cancer mortality. Women who examined their breasts visually, used their finger pads for palpation, and used their three middle fingers had a lower breast cancer mortality rate.[<a href="#cit/section_5.17">

373017</a>

3731]</p>

3732</section>

3733<section id="_201">

3734<h3 id="_201_toc">

3735Tissue Sampling (Fine-Needle Aspiration, Nipple Aspirate, Ductal Lavage)</h3>

3736<p id="_202" tabindex="-1">

3737Various methods to analyze breast tissue for malignancy have been proposed to screen for breast cancer, but none have been associated with mortality reduction.</p>

3738</section>

3815 <section id="_377">

3816

3817 <h2>

3818Appendix of Randomized Controlled Trials</h2>

3819

3820 <div id="_section_377" class="pdq-sections">

3821<p id="_73" tabindex="-1">

3822<strong>

3823 Health Insurance Plan, United States 1963 </strong>

3824[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

38251</a>

3826,<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

38272</a>

3828]

3829</p>

3830<div class="pdq-content-list">

3831<ul id="_378" class="pdq-address-block">

3832<li>

3833<strong>

3834Age at entry:</strong>

3835 40 to 64 years.</li>

3836<li>

3837<strong>

3838Randomization:</strong>

3839 Individual, but with significant imbalances in the

3840distribution of women between assigned arms, as evidenced by menopausal status (<em>

3841P</em>

3842 &lt; .0001)

3843and education (<em>

3844P</em>

3845 = .05).

3846</li>

3847<li>

3848<strong>

3849Sample size:</strong>

3850 30,000 to 31,092 in study group and 30,565 to 30,765 in control

3851group.</li>

3852<li>

3853<strong>

3854Consistency of reports:</strong>

3855 Variation in sample size reports.

3856</li>

3857<li>

3858<strong>

3859Intervention:</strong>

3860 Annual two-view mammography (MMG) and clinical breast examination (CBE) for 3 years.

3861</li>

3862<li>

3863<strong>

3864Control:</strong>

3865 Usual care.

3866</li>

3867<li>

3868

3869<strong>

3870Compliance: </strong>

3871 Nonattenders to first screening (35% of the screened population)

3872were not reinvited.</li>

3873<li>

3874<strong>

3875Contamination:</strong>

3876 Screening MMG was not available outside the trial; frequency of CBE performance among control women is unknown.</li>

3877<li>

3878

3879<strong>

3880Cause of death attribution:</strong>

3881 Women who died of breast cancer that had been diagnosed before entry into the study were excluded from the comparison between the screening and control groups. However, these exclusions were determined differently within the two groups. Women in the screening

3882group were excluded based on determinations made during the study

3883period at their initial screening visits. These women were dropped

3884from all further consideration in the study. By design, controls did

3885not have regular clinic visits, so the prestudy cancer status of

3886control patients was not determined. When a control patient died and

3887her cause of death was determined to be breast cancer, a

3888retrospective examination was made to determine the date of diagnosis

3889of her disease. If the date preceded the study period, the control patient was

3890excluded from the analysis. This difference in methodology has the

3891potential for a substantial bias when comparing breast cancer mortality

3892between the two groups, and this bias is likely to favor screening.</li>

3893<li>

3894<strong>

3895Analysis:</strong>

3896 Follow-up.</li>

3897<li>

3898<strong>

3899External audit:</strong>

3900 No.

3901</li>

3902<li>

3903<strong>

3904Follow-up duration:</strong>

3905 18 years.</li>

3906<li>

3907<strong>

3908Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% confidence interval [CI]):</strong>

3909 0.71

3910(0.55&#8211;0.93) at 10 years and 0.77 (0.61&#8211;0.97) at 15 years.

3911</li>

3912<li>

3913<strong>

3914Comments:</strong>

3915 The MMGs were of poor quality compared with those of later

3916trials, because of outdated equipment and techniques. The intervention consisted of both MMG and CBE. Major concerns about trial

3917performance are the validity of the initial randomization and the differential

3918exclusion of women with a prior history of breast cancer.

3919</li>

3920</ul>

3921</div>

3922<p id="_379" tabindex="-1">

3923<strong>

3924Malmo, Sweden 1976</strong>

3925 [<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

39263</a>

39284</a>

3929]

3930</p>

3931<div class="pdq-content-list">

3932<ul id="_113" class="pdq-address-block">

3933<li>

3934 <strong>

3935Age at entry:</strong>

3936 45 to 69 years.</li>

3937<li>

3938

3939<strong>

3940Randomization:</strong>

3941 Individual, within each birth-year cohort for the first phase,

3942MMG screening trial (MMST I). Individual for the entire birth cohort 1933 to 1945 for MMST II but with

3943variations imposed by limited resources. Validation by analysis of age in both

3944groups shows no significant difference.</li>

3945<li>

3946<strong>

3947Exclusions:</strong>

3948 In a Swedish meta-analysis, there were 393 women with preexisting

3949breast cancer excluded from the intervention group and 412 from the control

3950group. Overall, however, 86 more women were excluded from the

3951intervention group than from the control group.</li>

3952<li>

3953<strong>

3954Sample size:</strong>

3955

3956 21,088 study and 21,195 control.</li>

3957<li>

3958

3959<strong>

3960Consistency of reports:</strong>

3961 No variation in patient numbers.</li>

3962<li>

3963<strong>

3964Intervention:</strong>

3965 Two-view MMG every 18 to 24 months &#215; 5.</li>

3966<li>

3967

3968<strong>

3969Control:</strong>

3970 Usual care, with MMG at study end.</li>

3971<li>

3972<strong>

3973Compliance:</strong>

3974 Participants migrating from Malmo (2% per year) were not followed.

3975The participation rate of study women was 74% for the first round and 70% for

3976subsequent rounds.</li>

3977<li>

3978

3979<strong>

3980Contamination:</strong>

3981 24% of all control women had at least one MMG, as did 35% of the

3982control women aged 45 to 49 years.</li>

3983<li>

3984<strong>

3985Cause of death attribution:</strong>

3986 76% autopsy rate in early report, lower rate later.

3987Cause of death assessment blinded for women with a breast cancer diagnosis.

3988Linked to Swedish Cause of Death Registry.</li>

3989<li>

3990<strong>

3991Analysis:</strong>

3992 Evaluation, initially. Follow-up analysis, as part of the Swedish

3993meta-analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

39945</a>

3995]</li>

3996<li>

3997<strong>

3998External audit:</strong>

3999 No.</li>

4000<li>

4001<strong>

4002Follow-up duration: </strong>

400312 years.</li>

4004<li>

4005<strong>

4006Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4007 0.81

4008(0.62&#8211;1.07).</li>

4009<li>

4010<strong>

4011Comments:</strong>

4012 Evaluation analysis required a correction factor for the delay in the

4013performance of MMG in the control group. The two Malmo trials, MMST I and MMST II,

4014have been combined for most analyses.</li>

4015</ul>

4017<p id="_380" tabindex="-1">

4018<strong>

4019&#214;sterg&#246;tland (County E of Two-County Trial), Sweden 1977</strong>

4020 [<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

40216</a>

4022-<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

40238</a>

4024]

4025</p>

4026<div class="pdq-content-list">

4027<ul id="_381" class="pdq-address-block">

4028<li>

4029<strong>

4030Age at entry:</strong>

4031 40 to 74 years.</li>

4032<li>

4033

4034<strong>

4035Randomization:</strong>

4036 Geographic cluster, with stratification for residence (urban or

4037rural), socioeconomic factors, and size. Baseline breast cancer incidence and

4038mortality were comparable between the randomly assigned geographic clusters.

4039The study women were older than the control women, <em>

4040P</em>

4041 &lt; .0001, which would not have had a major effect on the outcome of the trial. </li>

4042<li>

4043<strong>

4044Exclusions:</strong>

4045 Women with preexisting breast cancer were excluded from both

4046groups, but the numbers were reported differently in different publications.

4047The Swedish meta-analysis excluded all women with a prior breast cancer

4048diagnosis, regardless of group assignment.</li>

4049<li>

4050<strong>

4051Sample size:</strong>

4052 Variably reported, ranging from 38,405 to 39,034 in the study and from

405337,145 to 37,936 in the control.</li>

4054<li>

4055<strong>

4056Consistency of reports:</strong>

4057 Variable.</li>

4058<li>

4059<strong>

4060Intervention:</strong>

4061 Three single-view MMGs every 2 years for women younger than 50 years and every 33 months

4062for women 50 years and older.</li>

4063<li>

4064<strong>

4065Control:</strong>

4066 Usual care, with MMG at study end.</li>

4067<li>

4068<strong>

4069Compliance:</strong>

4070 89% screened.</li>

4071<li>

4072<strong>

4073Contamination:</strong>

4074 13% of women in the Two-County trial had MMG as part of routine care,

4075mostly in 1983 and 1984.</li>

4076<li>

4077

4078<strong>

4079Cause of death attribution:</strong>

4080 Determined by a team of local physicians. When

4081results were recalculated in the Swedish meta-analysis, using data from the

4082Swedish Cause of Death Registry, there was less benefit for screening than had

4083been previously reported.</li>

4084<li>

4085<strong>

4086Analysis:</strong>

4087 Evaluation initially, with correction for delay in control group MMG.

4088Follow-up analysis, as part of the Swedish meta-analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

40895</a>

4090]</li>

4091<li>

4092<strong>

4093External audit:</strong>

4094 No. However, breast cancer cases and deaths were adjudicated by a Swedish panel that included the trial's investigators.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

40959</a>

4096]</li>

4097<li>

4098<strong>

4099Follow-up duration:</strong>

4100 12 years.</li>

4101<li>

4102<strong>

4103Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4104 0.82

4105(0.64&#8211;1.05), &#214;sterg&#246;tland.</li>

4106<li>

4107

4108<strong>

4109Comments:</strong>

4110 Concerns were raised about the randomization methodology and the

4111evaluation analysis, which required a correction for late performance of the

4112control group MMG. The Swedish meta-analysis resolved these questions

4113appropriately.</li>

4114</ul>

4203<strong>

4204Edinburgh, United Kingdom 1976 </strong>

4205 [<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

420610</a>

4207]

4208</p>

4209<div class="pdq-content-list">

4210<ul id="_385" class="pdq-address-block">

4211<li>

4212<strong>

4213Age at entry:</strong>

4214 45 to 64 years.</li>

4215<li>

4216

4217<strong>

4218Randomization:</strong>

4219 Cluster by physician practices, though many randomization

4220assignments were changed after study start. Within each practice, there was

4221inconsistent recruitment of women, according to the physician&#8217;s judgment about

4222each woman&#8217;s suitability for the trial. Large differences in socioeconomic

4223status between practices were not recognized until after the study end.</li>

4224<li>

4225<strong>

4226Exclusions:</strong>

4227 More women (338) with preexisting breast cancer were excluded

4228from the intervention group than from the control group (177).</li>

4229<li>

4230

4231<strong>

4232Sample size:</strong>

4233 23,226 study and 21,904 control.</li>

4234<li>

4235

4236<strong>

4237Consistency of reports:</strong>

4238 Good.</li>

4239<li>

4240<strong>

4241Intervention:</strong>

4242 Initially, two-view MMG and CBE; then annual CBE, with single-view MMG

4243in years 3, 5, and 7.</li>

4244<li>

4245

4246<strong>

4247Control:</strong>

4248 Usual care.</li>

4249<li>

4250

4251<strong>

4252Compliance:</strong>

4253 61% screened.</li>

4254<li>

4255

4256<strong>

4257Contamination:</strong>

4258 None.</li>

4259<li>

4260<strong>

4261Cause of death attribution:</strong>

4262

4263Cancer Registry Data.</li>

4264<li>

4265

4266<strong>

4267Analysis:</strong>

4268 Follow-up.</li>

4269<li>

4270<strong>

4271External audit:</strong>

4272

4273No.</li>

4274<li>

4275<strong>

4276Follow-up duration:</strong>

4277 10 years.</li>

4278<li>

4279

4280<strong>

4281Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4282 0.84

4283(0.63&#8211;1.12).</li>

4284<li>

4285<strong>

4286Comments:</strong>

4287 Randomization process was flawed. Socioeconomic differences between

4288study and control groups probably account for the higher all-cause mortality in control

4289women compared with screened women. This difference in all-cause mortality was four

4290times greater than the breast cancer mortality in the control group, and

4291therefore, may account for the higher breast cancer mortality in the control group

4292compared with screened women. Although a correction factor was used in the

4293final analysis, this may not adjust the analysis sufficiently.</li>

4294</ul>

4296<p id="_386" tabindex="-1">

4297The study design and conduct make these results difficult to assess or combine

4298with the results of other trials.

4300<p id="_387" tabindex="-1">

4301<strong>

4302National Breast Screening Study (NBSS)-1, Canada 1980 </strong>

4303

4304[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

430511</a>

4306]</p>

4307<div class="pdq-content-list">

4308<ul id="_388" class="pdq-address-block">

4309<li>

4310<strong>

4311Age at entry:</strong>

4312 40 to 49 years.</li>

4313<li>

4314

4315<strong>

4316Randomization:</strong>

4317 Individual volunteers, with names entered successively on

4318allocation lists. Although criticisms of the randomization procedure have

4319been made, a thorough independent review found no evidence of subversion and

4320that subversion on a scale large enough to affect the results was unlikely.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

432112</a>

4322]</li>

4323<li>

4324<strong>

4325Exclusions:</strong>

4326

4327Few, balanced between groups.</li>

4328<li>

4329

4330<strong>

4331Sample size:</strong>

4332 25,214 study (100% screened after entry CBE) and 25,216 control.</li>

4333<li>

4334<strong>

4335Consistency of reports:</strong>

4336

4337 Good.</li>

4338<li>

4339

4340<strong>

4341Intervention:</strong>

4342 Annual two-view MMG and CBE for 4 to 5 years.</li>

4343<li>

4344

4345<strong>

4346Control:</strong>

4347 Usual care.</li>

4348<li>

4349

4350<strong>

4351Compliance:</strong>

4352 Initially 100%, decreased to 85.5% by screen five.</li>

4353<li>

4354

4355<strong>

4356Contamination:</strong>

4357 26.4% in usual care group.</li>

4358<li>

4359<strong>

4360Cause of death attribution:</strong>

4361

4362 Death certificates, with review of questionable

4363cases by a blinded review panel. Also linked with the Canadian Mortality Data

4364Base, Statistics Canada.</li>

4365<li>

4366

4367<strong>

4368Analysis:</strong>

4369 Follow-up.</li>

4370<li>

4371<strong>

4372External audit:</strong>

4373

4374 Yes. Independent, with analysis of data by several reviewers.</li>

4375<li>

4376<strong>

4377Follow-up duration:</strong>

4378 25 years.</li>

4379<li>

4380<strong>

4381Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4382

4383

43841.09 (0.80&#8211;1.49).</li>

4385<li>

4386<strong>

4387Comments:</strong>

4388

4389 This is the only trial specifically designed to study women aged 40

4390to 49 years. Cancers diagnosed at entry in both study and control groups were

4391included. Concerns were expressed before the completion of the trial about

4392the technical adequacy of the MMGs, the training of the radiologists, and the

4393standardization of the equipment, which prompted an independent external

4394review. The primary deficiency identified by this review was the use of the

4395mediolateral view from 1980 to 1985 instead of the mediolateral oblique view,

4396which was used after 1985.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

439713</a>

4398] Subsequent analyses found the size and stage

4399of the cancers detected mammographically in this trial to be equivalent to

4400those of other trials.[<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

440114</a>

4402] This trial and NBSS-2 differ from the other randomized controlled trials (RCTs) in

4403the consistent use of adjuvant hormone therapy and chemotherapy following local breast

4404cancer therapy in women with axillary node-positive disease.</li>

4405</ul>

4580<p id="_392" tabindex="-1">

4581<strong>

4582Gothenburg, Sweden 1982</strong>

4583</p>

4584<div class="pdq-content-list">

4585<ul id="_393" class="pdq-address-block">

4586<li>

4587<strong>

4588Age at entry:</strong>

4589 39 to 59 years.</li>

4590<li>

4591<strong>

4592Randomization:</strong>

4593 Complex; cluster randomly assigned within birth year by day of birth for older group (aged 50&#8211;59 years) and by individual for younger group (aged 39&#8211;49 years); ratio of study to control varied by year depending on MMG availability (randomization took place, 1982&#8211;1984).</li>

4594<li>

4595<strong>

4596Exclusions:</strong>

4597

4598A similar proportion of women were excluded from both groups for

4599prior breast cancer diagnosis (1.2% each).</li>

4600<li>

4601<strong>

4602Sample size:</strong>

4603 Most recent publication: 21,650 invited; 29,961 controls.</li>

4604<li>

4605

4606<strong>

4607Consistency of reports:</strong>

4608 Variable.</li>

4609<li>

4610

4611<strong>

4612Intervention:</strong>

4613 Initial two-view MMG, then single-view MMG every 18 months &#215; 4. Single-read first three rounds, then double-read.</li>

4614<li>

4615<strong>

4616Control:</strong>

4617 Control group received one screening exam approximately 3 to 8 months after the final screen in study group.</li>

4618<li>

4619<strong>

4620Cause of death attribution:</strong>

4621 Linked to Swedish Cause of Death Registry; also used an independent end point committee.</li>

4622<li>

4623<strong>

4624Analysis:</strong>

4625 Both evaluation and follow-up methods.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

46265</a>

4627]</li>

4628<li>

4629<strong>

4630External audit:</strong>

4631 No.</li>

4632<li>

4633<strong>

4634Follow-up duration:</strong>

4635 12 to 14 years.</li>

4636<li>

4637<strong>

4638Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4639 Aged 39 to 59 years: 0.79 (0.58&#8211;1.08) [evaluation]; 0.77 (0.60&#8211;1.00) [follow-up].</li>

4640<li>

4641<strong>

4642Comments:</strong>

4643 No reduction for women aged 50 to 54 years, but similar reductions for other 5-year age groups.

4644</li>

4645<li>

4646<strong>

4647Conclusions:</strong>

4648 Delay in the performance of MMG in the control group and unequal numbers of women in invited and control groups (complex randomization process) complicates interpretation. </li>

4649</ul>

4651<p id="_375" tabindex="-1">

4652<strong>

4653AGE Trial</strong>

4654 [<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

465517</a>

4656,<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

465718</a>

4658]</p>

4659<div class="pdq-content-list">

4660<ul id="_376" class="pdq-address-block">

4661<li>

4662<strong>

4663Age at entry:</strong>

4664 39 to 41 years.</li>

4665<li>

4666<strong>

4667Randomization:</strong>

4668 Individuals from lists of general practitioners in geographically defined areas of England, Wales, and Scotland; allocation was concealed.</li>

4669<li>

4670<strong>

4671Exclusions:</strong>

4672

4673Small (n = 30 in invited group and n = 51 in not invited group) number excluded in each group because individuals could not be located or were deceased.</li>

4674<li>

4675<strong>

4676Sample size:</strong>

4677 160,921 (53,884 invited; 106,956 not invited).</li>

4678<li>

4679

4680<strong>

4681Consistency of reports:</strong>

4682 Not applicable.</li>

4683<li>

4684

4685<strong>

4686Intervention:</strong>

4687 Invited group aged 48 years and younger were offered annual screening by MMG (double-view first screen, then single mediolateral oblique view thereafter); 68% accepted first screening and 69% to 70% were reinvited (81% attended at least one screen).</li>

4688<li>

4689<strong>

4690Control:</strong>

4691 Those who were not invited received usual medical care, unaware of their participation, and few were screened before randomization.</li>

4692<li>

4693<strong>

4694Cause of death attribution:</strong>

4695 From the National Health Service (NHS) central register, death certificate code accepted.</li>

4696<li>

4697<strong>

4698Analysis:</strong>

4699 Follow-up method was intention-to-treat (although all women aged 50 years would be offered screening by NHS).</li>

4700<li>

4701<strong>

4702External audit:</strong>

4703 None.</li>

4704<li>

4705<strong>

4706Follow-up duration:</strong>

4707 10.7 years.</li>

4708<li>

4709<strong>

4710Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4711 0.83 (0.66&#8211;1.04).</li>

4712<li>

4713<strong>

4714Conclusions:</strong>

4715 Not a statistically significant result but fits with other studies.</li>

4716<li>

4717<strong>

4718Follow-up duration:</strong>

4719 Restricted to 10 years from randomization.</li>

4720<li>

4721<strong>

4722Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4723 0.75 (0.58&#8211;0.97).</li>

4724<li>

4725<strong>

4726Conclusions:</strong>

4727 A statistically significant result.</li>

4728<li>

4729<strong>

4730Follow-up duration:</strong>

4731 Median 17.7 years.</li>

4732<li>

4733<strong>

4734Relative risk of breast cancer death, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4735 0.88 (0.74&#8211;1.04).</li>

4736<li>

4737<strong>

4738Conclusions:</strong>

4739 Not a statistically significant result.</li>

4740<li>

4741<strong>

4742Follow-up duration:</strong>

4743 Median 17.7 years.</li>

4744<li>

4745<strong>

4746Relative risk of all-cause mortality, screening versus control (95% CI):</strong>

4747 0.98 (0.93&#8211;1.03).</li>

4748<li>

4749<strong>

4750Conclusions:</strong>

4751 Not a statistically significant result.</li>

4752</ul>

4753</div>

4754<p id="_818" tabindex="-1">

4755The United Kingdom Age Trial, a large RCT, compared the effect of mammographic screening on breast cancer mortality in women invited for annual mammography aged 40 years and older when compared with NHS screening programs that began at age 50 years. The primary end point of the AGE Trial was mortality from breast cancer diagnosed during the intervention period until immediately before participants&#8217; first NHS screening. This trial remains the only trial designed specifically to study the effect of mammographic screening starting at age 40 years and is one of three RCTs, which the Cochran group&#8217;s 2013 meta-analysis deemed adequately randomized.</p>

4756<p id="_819" tabindex="-1">

4757In 2006, the AGE Trial published results of breast cancer mortality at a mean follow-up at 10.7 years: a reduction in breast cancer mortality in the intervention group, which did not reach statistical significance (105 breast cancer deaths in intervention group vs. 251 breast cancer death in control group).</p>

4758<p id="_820" tabindex="-1">

4759In 2015, the AGE Trial published results of breast cancer mortality at a median follow-up of 17.7 years: no statistically significant reduction after more than 10 years of follow-up and no statistically significant decrease in all-cause mortality. At this time, it also published results of a reanalysis of the original data set: a small, transient, statistically significant reduction in breast cancer mortality in the intervention group during the first 10 years after randomization (83 breast cancer deaths in intervention group vs. 219 breast cancer death in control group). </p>

4760<p id="_821" tabindex="-1">

4761In 2020, the AGE Trial published final results based on median follow-up of 22.9 years including:</p>

4762<div class="pdq-content-list">

4763<ol id="_822">

4764<li>

4765Positive effect in the first 10 years after randomization. The absolute difference in breast cancer mortality was -0.6 deaths per 1,667 women in the 40 to 49 years age group; 1,150 women would need to be screened to prevent one breast cancer death in this age group. A post hoc analysis showed that years of life lost caused by breast cancer mortality were 67.4 out of 1,000 women in the intervention group versus 78.9 out of 1,000 women in the control group; this is equivalent to 11.5 years of life saved per 1,000 women invited to screening in the intervention group and a total of 620 years of life saved.</li>

4766<li>

4767There was no statistically significant reduction in breast cancer mortality or all-cause mortality in the intervention group compared with the control group.</li>

4768<li>

4769In the intervention group, 18.1% of women had at least one false-positive result.</li>

4770</ol>

4771</div>

4772<p id="_823" tabindex="-1">

4773This evidence is inadequate to support the conclusion of a clinically significant breast cancer mortality reduction attributable to initiation of screening mammography among women aged 39 to 49 years. The reported mortality reduction is a small, transient reduction in breast cancer mortality based on post hoc, subset analysis, nonstandard imaging protocol, and nonstandard threshold for biopsy (microcalcifications were not biopsied). In absolute terms, the difference in breast cancer mortality was -0.6 deaths per 1,667 women in the 40 to 49 years age group based on a reanalysis of the original data set, which was not statistically significant, and the recalculation of breast cancer mortality in a subgroup restricted to 10 years of follow-up. At a median follow-up of 22.9 years, there was no statistically significant decrease in risk of breast cancer or all-cause mortality.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

477418</a>

4775]</p>

4776<p id="_824" tabindex="-1">

4777This evidence is inadequate to make a clear determination of the magnitude of overdiagnosis. Because the evidence is based on subgroup analysis and nonstandard imaging schedule, nonstandard imaging protocol, and a nonstandard threshold for biopsy (microcalcifications were not biopsied) with uncertain relevance to the general population, it does not support the investigators' conclusion of &#8220;at worst a small amount of overdiagnosis."[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

477818</a>

4779]</p>

4907 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

4908

4909 <h2>

4910About This PDQ Summary</h2>

4911

4912 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

4913<section id="_AboutThis_2">

4914<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

4915Purpose of This Summary</h3>

4916<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

4917This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about breast cancer screening. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

4918</section>

4919<section id="_AboutThis_4">

4920<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

4921Reviewers and Updates</h3>

4922<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

4923This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/screening-prevention" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/screening-prevention">

4924PDQ Screening and Prevention Editorial Board</a>

4925, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

4926<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

4927 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

4928<div class="pdq-content-list">

4929<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

4930<li>

4931be discussed at a meeting,</li>

4932<li>

4933be cited with text, or</li>

4934<li>

4935replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

4936</ul>

4937</div>

4938<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

4939Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

4940<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

4941Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

4942Email Us</a>

4943. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

4944</section>

4945<section id="_AboutThis_10">

4946<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

4947Levels of Evidence</h3>

4948<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

4949Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Screening and Prevention Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/screening-prevention">

4950formal evidence ranking system</a>

4951 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

4981Contact Us</h3>

4983More information about contacting us or receiving help with the Cancer.gov website can be found on our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact">

4984Contact Us for Help</a>

4985 page. Questions can also be submitted to Cancer.gov through the website&#8217;s <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

4986Email Us</a>

4987.</p>
